import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import math


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Column-wise gated self-attention over MSA representations.
    For each residue position, attention is computed across the MSA depth
    (sequence) dimension.  This lets the model share information between
    different aligned sequences at the same position.
    """

    def __init__(self, msa_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0):
        super().__init__()
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads
        assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0

        self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)
        self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)

    def forward(self, msa_repr: torch.Tensor, msa_mask: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            msa_repr: (B, S, N, msa_dim)
            msa_mask: (B, S, N)
        Returns:
            updated MSA (B, S, N, msa_dim)
        """
        B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape
        h = self.num_heads
        d = self.head_dim

        # Transpose so column (residue position) is the batch dim for attention
        x = msa_repr.permute(0, 2, 1, 3)  # (B, N, S, D)
        x = self.layer_norm(x)

        q = self.query(x).view(B, N, S, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        k = self.key(x).view(B, N, S, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        v = self.value(x).view(B, N, S, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, N, S, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)

        attn = torch.einsum("bnhid,bnhjd->bnhij", q, k) / math.sqrt(d)

        col_mask = msa_mask.permute(0, 2, 1)  # (B, N, S)
        mask = col_mask.view(B, N, 1, 1, S).expand_as(attn)
        attn = attn.masked_fill(mask == 0, float("-inf"))
        attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)
        attn = self.dropout(attn)

        out = torch.einsum("bnhij,bnhjd->bnhid", attn, v)
        out = g * out
        out = out.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).reshape(B, N, S, D)
        out = self.out_proj(out)
        return out.permute(0, 2, 1, 3)  # back to (B, S, N, D)


msa_dim = 64
num_heads = 4
seq_len = 32
msa_depth = 8
batch_size = 2


def get_inputs():
    msa_repr = torch.randn(batch_size, msa_depth, seq_len, msa_dim)
    msa_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, msa_depth, seq_len)
    return [msa_repr, msa_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [msa_dim, num_heads]

Pass it to kernel. Also, we need to apply the gate bias. The gate weight is (D, D), bias is (D). Layer norm: In the reference, x is layer_norm(x). The layer norm weight and bias are parameters of the module. We need to apply layer norm before the kernel, or fuse it. Since fusing layer norm is extra complexity, we can apply it in Python: `x = self.layer_norm(msa_repr.permute(0,2,1,3))`. Then call the kernel with x. That avoids writing a custom layer norm kernel. The reference's first operation is permute + layer_norm. PyTorch's layer_norm kernel is efficient. So it's fine to keep it in Python. Then our custom kernel handles the rest: Q,K,V,G, attention, gate multiply, out_proj, permute back. But wait: the reference does `x = msa_repr.permute(0,2,1,3)` and then `x = self.layer_norm(x)`. In Python, we can do the same: compute x = msa_repr.permute(0,2,1,3); x = self.layer_norm(x). Then call our kernel. But maybe we can fuse permute into the kernel by reading msa_repr directly. However, reading with stride is less efficient. Since we already have memory bandwidth, doing the permute in PyTorch (which might be a view if contiguous? Actually permute is not contiguous; layer_norm might require contiguous memory. So PyTorch will allocate a new tensor for x after layer_norm. So x is contiguous (B,N,S,D). Good. Then our kernel reads x contiguous. So ModelNew.forward: ```python def forward(self, msa_repr, msa_mask): B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape # transpose and layer norm (same as reference) x = msa_repr.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() x = self.layer_norm(x) col_mask = msa_mask.permute(0, 2, 1).contiguous() out = torch.empty(B, S, N, D, device=msa_repr.device, dtype=msa_repr.dtype) # launch kernel self.kernel.forward_cuda( x, col_mask, self.w_q, self.w_k, self.w_v, self.w_g, self.b_g, self.w_out, self.b_out, out, B, N, S, D, self.num_heads ) return out ``` But we need to pre-transpose weights. In __init__, after copying parameters (or using the same parameters), we can create transposed weights. Wait, we need to accept the same constructor arguments and produce numerically equivalent output. We can define ModelNew(nn.Module) that initializes the same submodules (layer_norm, query, key, value, gate, out_proj, dropout). Then in forward, call the custom kernel. We don't have to use the same submodules for computation, but we should copy their weights. Or we can just call the custom extension that uses the module's parameters. Better approach: In ModelNew.__init__, call the same initializations as Model. Then set `self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)`. In forward, compute x = msa_repr.permute(0,2,1,3); x = self.layer_norm(x). Then compute Q,K,V,G using the custom kernel. But we need to extract the weights. For self.query, self.key, etc., we can pass their weight tensors to the kernel. In PyTorch, `self.query.weight` is shape (out_features, in_features). We can transpose it on host side: `self.query.weight.t().contiguous()`. But transposing creates a copy each time, which is expensive. We should precompute transposed weights as buffers in __init__ or as registered buffers. But the parameters need to remain nn.Parameter for training (though training is not evaluated). To be safe, we can register them as buffers or compute them lazily. Since the weights don't change during inference, we can do in __init__: ```python self.register_buffer('w_q', self.query.weight.t().contiguous()) self.register_buffer('w_k', self.key.weight.t().contiguous()) ... ``` But if the underlying weight is updated (e.g., during training), the buffer won't update. However, for inference only, it's fine. But the problem statement says replace forward computation; it doesn't mention training. We just need to produce numerically equivalent output. We can keep the original modules and in forward compute transposed weights on the fly, but that overhead is small compared to kernel. Or we can just pass the original weights and do transposed access in the kernel. Let's check whether transposed access can be coalesced. Original weight shape: (out_features, in_features) = (64, 64). In kernel, if we use `w_q[in*64 + out]`, then for a fixed out and varying in, the access is strided by 64 floats. That's not coalesced across threads. So we should transpose. Let's do dynamic shared memory: ```cpp extern __shared__ float smem[]; ``` Partition: - x_shared: size S*D - q_shared: size H*S*d - k_shared: size H*S*d - v_shared: size H*S*d - g_shared: size H*S*d - attn_shared: size H*S*S - out_shared: size H*S*d Total = S*D + 4*H*S*d + H*S*S + H*S*d Since D = H*d, 4*H*S*d = 4*S*D. H*S*d = S*D. So total = S*D + 4*S*D + H*S*S + S*D = 6*S*D + H*S*S. For S=8,D=64,H=4: 6*8*64 + 4*8*8 = 3072 + 256 = 3328 floats = 13.3KB. Fine. We can compute offsets: ```cpp float* x_shared = smem; float* q_shared = x_shared + S*D; float* k_shared = q_shared + H*S*d; float* v_shared = k_shared + H*S*d; float* g_shared = v_shared + H*S*d; float* attn_shared = g_shared + H*S*d; float* out_shared = attn_shared + H*S*S; ``` Now indexing functions: ```cpp inline __device__ float& x_s(float* x, int s, int k, int S) { return x[k*S + s]; } inline __device__ float& q_s(float* q, int h, int s, int d_, int S, int d) { return q[(h*S + s)*d + d_]; } ... ``` But better to inline compute manually to avoid function call overhead. Now kernel: ```cpp __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ mask, // col_mask (B,N,S) const float* __restrict__ w_q, // (D, D) transposed const float* __restrict__ w_k, const float* __restrict__ w_v, const float* __restrict__ w_g, const float* __restrict__ b_g, const float* __restrict__ w_out, // (D, D) transposed const float* __restrict__ b_out, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int S, int D, int H ) { int d = D / H; int bn = blockIdx.x; int b = bn / N; int n = bn % N; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; if (num_threads < 128) { // but we can set blockDim.x = 128 always } // shared memory offsets extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_shared = smem; float* q_shared = x_shared + S * D; float* k_shared = q_shared + H * S * d; float* v_shared = k_shared + H * S * d; float* g_shared = v_shared + H * S * d; float* attn_shared = g_shared + H * S * d; float* out_shared = attn_shared + H * S * S; // Load x into shared memory transposed: x_shared[k*S + s] const float* x_ptr = x + bn * S * D; for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += num_threads) { int s = idx / D; int k = idx % D; x_shared[k * S + s] = x_ptr[s * D + k]; } __syncthreads(); // Step 1: Compute Q, K, V, G // Each thread computes up to ceil(H*S*d / num_threads) elements per matrix? Actually we do 4 matrices in one go. // Total elements per matrix: H*S*d. For H*S*d = 512. With 128 threads, each thread does 4. // We'll loop over the 4 matrices using the same index mapping. for (int idx = tid; idx < H * S * d; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * d); int rem = idx % (S * d); int s = rem / d; int dd = rem % d; float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f, sum_g = 0.0f; // dot product over D #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xv = x_shared[k * S + s]; int widx = k * D + h * d + dd; sum_q += xv * w_q[widx]; sum_k += xv * w_k[widx]; sum_v += xv * w_v[widx]; sum_g += xv * w_g[widx]; } q_shared[idx] = sum_q; k_shared[idx] = sum_k; v_shared[idx] = sum_v; // gate with bias and sigmoid float g_val = sum_g + b_g[h * d + dd]; g_shared[idx] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g_val)); } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: Compute attention scores // Each thread handles one or more (h,i,j) int total_attn = H * S * S; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_attn; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * S); int rem = idx % (S * S); int i = rem / S; int j = rem % S; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; ++dd) { sum += q_shared[(h * S + i) * d + dd] * k_shared[(h * S + j) * d + dd]; } attn_shared[idx] = sum / sqrtf((float)d); } __syncthreads(); // Step 3: Apply mask and softmax // Load mask for this (b,n) const float* mask_ptr = mask + bn * S; float mask_vals[8]; // S max 8, dynamic? Use shared memory for mask. // Actually we can just read from global memory in the loop. // For each row i (0..S-1), we need max and sum. // Let's have each thread process some rows. for (int idx = tid; idx < H * S; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / S; int i = idx % S; // compute max float max_val = -1e9f; // or -FLT_MAX #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = -INFINITY; // or -1e9? The reference uses -inf. if (val > max_val) max_val = val; // store masked value back? We can store temporarily in registers, but we need to share across j. } // compute sum of exp float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = -INFINITY; sum_exp += expf(val - max_val); } // normalize float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = -INFINITY; attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j] = expf(val - max_val) * inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Step 4: Weighted sum with V, gate multiply for (int idx = tid; idx < H * S * d; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * d); int rem = idx % (S * d); int i = rem / d; int dd = rem % d; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { sum += attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j] * v_shared[(h * S + j) * d + dd]; } out_shared[idx] = sum * g_shared[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Step 5: Output projection // Output layout: (B, S, N, D) // We process each output element (s, d_out) // Total S*D = 512. for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += num_threads) { int s = idx / D; int d_out = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { int h = d_in / d; int dd = d_in % d; float val = out_shared[(h * S + s) * d + dd]; sum += val * w_out[d_in * D + d_out]; } sum += b_out[d_out]; int out_idx = ((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + d_out; out[out_idx] = sum; } } ``` This is a single kernel that does everything after layer norm. For fixed s, `out_shared[(h*S+s)*d + dd]` as `d_in` goes 0..63 maps to `h=0, dd=0..15`, then `h=1, dd=0..15`, etc. So memory access is sequential across `d_in`. Good. Threads in a warp have same `s` but different `d_out`. For a fixed `d_in`, all threads load the same `out_shared` element? No, `out_shared` depends on `h` and `dd`, not on `d_out`. So for a given `d_in`, all threads load the same `out_shared` value! Because `h = d_in / d`, `dd = d_in % d`. So for fixed `d_in`, all threads load `val = out_shared[(h*S+s)*d + dd]`. That's a broadcast from shared memory. No bank conflict: all threads read the same address, which is broadcast. Then they load `w_out[d_in * D + d_out]`. Here `d_out` varies across threads (consecutive). So memory is contiguous. Coalesced. Great. So step 5 is very efficient. Now about step 1: We compute Q,K,V,G in the same loop. But we store all four to shared memory. For each thread, we compute four dot products using the same `x_shared` value. This is good because `x_shared` is reused. But reading `w_q`, `w_k`, `w_v`, `w_g` from global memory might be the bottleneck. Each weight matrix is 64x64 = 4KB. Four matrices = 16KB. For 64 blocks, total weight reads from HBM = 64*16KB = 1MB. That's fine. However, we read each weight matrix across all threads in a block. With 128 threads, each thread reads D floats from each weight matrix. Total per block: 128 threads * 64 floats * 4 matrices = 32768 weight reads. But each weight is read by multiple threads. Specifically, each weight element `w_q[k][out]` is read by all threads that compute that output. For a given output (h,s,dd), only one thread reads the row `w_q[:, h*d+dd]`. So each row is read by S threads? Wait, for a fixed out = h*d+dd, Q outputs for all s (0..7) compute using the same row. In our assignment, idx maps to (h,s,dd). For fixed h and dd, there are S=8 threads (s=0..7) that need the same row `w_q[:, h*d+dd]`. However, they don't read the row collaboratively; each thread reads the entire row independently. So each weight row is read 8 times from global memory (or L2). That's 8*16 rows * 64 floats = 8192 reads per matrix, times 4 = 32768 reads. Since the row is small (64 floats = 256 bytes), it may stay in L2 cache. The first thread reads it from HBM/L2, subsequent threads hit L2. So effective HBM traffic is 64 rows * 256 bytes = 16KB per matrix. Good. Thus step 1 is efficient. Now, we need to write the host-side wrapper. ```cpp torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor mask, torch::Tensor w_q, torch::Tensor w_k, torch::Tensor w_v, torch::Tensor w_g, torch::Tensor b_g, torch::Tensor w_out, torch::Tensor b_out, int num_heads ) { int B = x.size(0); int N = x.size(1); int S = x.size(2); int D = x.size(3); int H = num_heads; int d = D / H; auto out = torch::empty({B, S, N, D}, x.options()); int total_blocks = B * N; int threads = 128; size_t smem_size = (6 * S * D + H * S * S) * sizeof(float); fused_attention_kernel<<<total_blocks, threads, smem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), mask.data_ptr<float>(), w_q.data_ptr<float>(), w_k.data_ptr<float>(), w_v.data_ptr<float>(), w_g.data_ptr<float>(), b_g.data_ptr<float>(), w_out.data_ptr<float>(), b_out.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, S, D, H ); return out; } ``` But we need to make sure inputs are contiguous and on CUDA. Let's draft the full kernel: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #ifndef M_SQRT1_2 #define M_SQRT1_2 0.70710678118654752440 #endif __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ mask, const float* __restrict__ w_q, const float* __restrict__ w_k, const float* __restrict__ w_v, const float* __restrict__ w_g, const float* __restrict__ b_g, const float* __restrict__ w_out, const float* __restrict__ b_out, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int S, int D, int H) { int d = D / H; int bn = blockIdx.x; int b = bn / N; int n = bn % N; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_shared = smem; float* q_shared = x_shared + S * D; float* k_shared = q_shared + H * S * d; float* v_shared = k_shared + H * S * d; float* g_shared = v_shared + H * S * d; float* attn_shared = g_shared + H * S * d; float* out_shared = attn_shared + H * S * S; const float* x_ptr = x + bn * S * D; for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += num_threads) { int s = idx / D; int k = idx % D; x_shared[k * S + s] = x_ptr[s * D + k]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q, K, V, G int total_proj = H * S * d; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_proj; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * d); int rem = idx % (S * d); int s = rem / d; int dd = rem % d; float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f, sum_g = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xv = x_shared[k * S + s]; int widx = k * D + h * d + dd; sum_q += xv * w_q[widx]; sum_k += xv * w_k[widx]; sum_v += xv * w_v[widx]; sum_g += xv * w_g[widx]; } q_shared[idx] = sum_q; k_shared[idx] = sum_k; v_shared[idx] = sum_v; float gv = sum_g + b_g[h * d + dd]; // sigmoid g_shared[idx] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gv)); } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores int total_attn = H * S * S; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_attn; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * S); int rem = idx % (S * S); int i = rem / S; int j = rem % S; float sum = 0.0f; for (int dd = 0; dd < d; ++dd) { sum += q_shared[(h * S + i) * d + dd] * k_shared[(h * S + j) * d + dd]; } attn_shared[idx] = sum / sqrtf((float)d); } __syncthreads(); // Apply mask and softmax const float* mask_ptr = mask + bn * S; for (int idx = tid; idx < H * S; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / S; int i = idx % S; float max_val = -1e30f; // close to -inf for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = -1e30f; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } // In the unlikely case all are masked, max_val stays -1e30f. // We want to mimic PyTorch's behavior: if max is -inf, output is nan. // Using -1e30f instead of true -inf will cause exp(0)=1 and uniform distribution. // That's wrong. Let's use true negative infinity. // ... Actually we can compute using float max_val = -INFINITY; but to be safe we use a very large negative. // Let's define a proper -inf. } } ``` Ah, I need to handle the `-inf` issue carefully. Since PyTorch uses `-inf`, and for partially masked rows, max_val is finite. The difference between `-1e30f` and `-INFINITY` doesn't matter if there is at least one unmasked element. But if all are masked, difference matters. Since mask may be all ones, it's okay, but we should still be correct. We can define: ```cpp const float NEG_INF = -1e30f; // not good for all-masked ``` Better: ```cpp const float NEG_INF = -CUDART_INF_F; ``` We need to include `<cuda_runtime_api.h>` or `<math_constants.h>`. Actually `CUDART_INF_F` is defined in `math_constants.h`. We can just compute: ```cpp const float NEG_INF = __int_as_float(0xFF800000); ``` Then: ```cpp float max_val = NEG_INF; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; sum_exp += expf(val - max_val); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j] = expf(val - max_val) * inv_sum; } ``` If all masked, max_val = NEG_INF. Then `val - max_val` is NaN (NEG_INF - NEG_INF = NaN). `expf(NaN)` = NaN. `sum_exp` = NaN. `inv_sum` = NaN. Output = NaN. Good. Now, the loop over `j` is done three times. For S=8, that's fine. We could unroll manually or with pragma. Now, step 5 (out_proj): ```cpp for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += num_threads) { int s = idx / D; int d_out = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { int h = d_in / d; int dd = d_in % d; float val = out_shared[(h * S + s) * d + dd]; sum += val * w_out[d_in * D + d_out]; } sum += b_out[d_out]; int out_idx = ((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + d_out; out[out_idx] = sum; } ``` All good. } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores int total_attn = H * S * S; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_attn; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * S); int rem = idx % (S * S); int i = rem / S; int j = rem % S; float sum = 0.0f; for (int dd = 0; dd < d; ++dd) { sum += q_shared[(h * S + i) * d + dd] * k_shared[(h * S + j) * d + dd]; } attn_shared[idx] = sum / sqrtf((float)d); } __syncthreads(); // Apply mask and softmax const float* mask_ptr = mask + bn * S; for (int idx = tid; idx < H * S; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / S; int i = idx % S; float max_val = NEG_INF; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; sum_exp += expf(val - max_val); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j] = expf(val - max_val) * inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Weighted sum with V and gate multiply for (int idx = tid; idx < total_proj; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * d); int rem = idx % (S * d); int i = rem / d; int dd = rem % d; float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { sum += attn_shared[(h * S + i) * S + j] * v_shared[(h * S + j) * d + dd]; } out_shared[idx] = sum * g_shared[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Output projection for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += num_threads) { int s = idx / D; int d_out = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { int h = d_in / d; int dd = d_in % d; float val = out_shared[(h * S + s) * d + dd]; sum += val * w_out[d_in * D + d_out]; } sum += b_out[d_out]; int out_idx = ((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + d_out; out[out_idx] = sum; } } torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor mask, torch::Tensor w_q, torch::Tensor w_k, torch::Tensor w_v, torch::Tensor w_g, torch::Tensor b_g, torch::Tensor w_out, torch::Tensor b_out, int num_heads ) { int B = x.size(0); int N = x.size(1); int S = x.size(2); int D = x.size(3); int H = num_heads; auto out = torch::empty({B, S, N, D}, x.options()); int threads = 128; int blocks = B * N; size_t smem_size = (6 * S * D + H * S * S) * sizeof(float); fused_attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), mask.data_ptr<float>(), w_q.data_ptr<float>(), w_k.data_ptr<float>(), w_v.data_ptr<float>(), w_g.data_ptr<float>(), b_g.data_ptr<float>(), w_out.data_ptr<float>(), b_out.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, S, D, H ); return out; } ''' ``` Now the Python class: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, msa_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0 self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim) self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) # compile self.fused_cuda = load_inline( name='fused_attention', cpp_sources=..., cuda_sources=cuda_src, functions=['forward_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], ) def forward(self, msa_repr, msa_mask): B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape x = msa_repr.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() x = self.layer_norm(x) col_mask = msa_mask.permute(0, 2, 1).float().contiguous() w_q = self.query.weight.t().contiguous() w_k = self.key.weight.t().contiguous() w_v = self.value.weight.t().contiguous() w_g = self.gate.weight.t().contiguous() b_g = self.gate.bias.contiguous() if self.gate.bias is not None else torch.zeros(D, device=msa_repr.device, dtype=msa_repr.dtype) w_out = self.out_proj.weight.t().contiguous() b_out = self.out_proj.bias.contiguous() if self.out_proj.bias is not None else torch.zeros(D, device=msa_repr.device, dtype=msa_repr.dtype) out = self.fused_cuda.forward_cuda( x, col_mask, w_q, w_k, w_v, w_g, b_g, w_out, b_out, self.num_heads ) return out ``` One issue: If `dropout.p > 0` and training is True, we are not applying dropout. But since we are fusing everything, we can't easily apply dropout after the kernel without pulling attn out. However, it's likely not needed. Another issue: For correctness, we might need to handle the fact that `nn.Linear` weights are transposed relative to what we think. In PyTorch, `nn.Linear` weight is `(out_features, in_features)`. So `self.query.weight` shape is `(D, D)`. `x @ self.query.weight.T` is implemented in PyTorch. In the kernel, we want to compute `sum_k x[s][k] * W_q[out][k]`. Since we transposed `W_q` to `(D, D)` as `W_q_T[k][out] = W_q[out][k]`, the access `w_q[k*D + out]` is correct. Let's double-check: `w_q = self.query.weight.t().contiguous()`. `self.query.weight` is `(D, D)`. `.t()` gives `(D, D)` with `w_q[k][out] = original_weight[out][k]`. `.contiguous()` ensures row-major. So `w_q[k*D + out]` is `original_weight[out][k]`. In kernel, `sum += x[s][k] * w_q[k*D + out]`. That matches `x[s] @ W_q^T`. Good. Similarly for gate: `sum_g += x[s][k] * w_g[k*D + out]`. Then add `b_g[out]`. Good. Now for out_proj: The reference does `out @ W_out^T + b_out`. `out` shape is `(S, D)`. `W_out` shape is `(D, D)`. So `out @ W_out.T` means `result[s][d_out] = sum_{d_in} out[s][d_in] * W_out[d_out][d_in]`. Wait careful: In PyTorch, `nn.Linear` computes `y = x @ W^T + b`. So for `out_proj`, input x_in is `(S, D)`, weight W_out is `(D, D)`. So output = x_in @ W_out^T + b. So `output[s][d_out] = sum_{d_in} x_in[s][d_in] * W_out[d_out][d_in]`. We have `w_out = self.out_proj.weight.t().contiguous()`. `self.out_proj.weight` shape `(D, D)`. `w_out` is transpose: `w_out[d_in][d_out] = W_out[d_out][d_in]`. Then in kernel, `sum += val * w_out[d_in * D + d_out]`. That matches. Good. Bias: `b_out[d_out]`. Good. One more thing: The reference applies layer norm to x before linear layers. Now, one more nuance: The reference's `softmax` operates on a tensor that may contain `-inf`. PyTorch's `F.softmax` uses a numerically stable algorithm: subtract max before exp. For rows with finite max, it works. For rows with all `-inf`, max is `-inf`, and subtraction yields NaN, resulting in NaN outputs. Our kernel does the same. But we must be careful about `expf(val - max_val)` when max_val is `-inf`. `val` is also `-inf`, so `-inf - (-inf)` is NaN. `expf(NaN)` is NaN. So sum_exp is NaN. `1.0f / sum_exp` is NaN. `expf(NaN) * NaN` is NaN. Good. But what if `max_val` is `-inf`? `if (val > max_val)` is false for val = -inf, because -inf > -inf is false. The indexing `((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + dd` is correct for row-major order of `(B,S,N,D)`. Now, consider numerical equivalence of the sigmoid. PyTorch's `sigmoid(x)` is `1 / (1 + exp(-x))`. We use `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gv))`. That's fine. Subtle difference: PyTorch might clamp for large values. If `gv` is large positive, `-gv` underflows in `expf`, giving 0, denominator = 1, result = 1. If `gv` is large negative, `expf(-gv)` overflows to inf, denominator = inf, result = 0. This matches PyTorch's behavior. However, for very large negative (e.g., -1000), `expf(1000)` overflow to inf. In PyTorch, `sigmoid(-1000)` returns 0.0. Our code returns `1 / (1 + inf) = 0`. Good. Wait, what about `expf(-gv)` where `gv` is large positive? `expf(-1000)` underflows to 0, `1/(1+0) = 1`. Good. What about `gv` = NaN? Then sigmoid = NaN. Same as PyTorch. Now, the softmax. PyTorch's `F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)` computes stable softmax. For each row, it subtracts max, exponentiates, sums, divides. That's what we do. However, PyTorch does it in an efficient tree reduction. Could there be differences? Yes, but within tolerance. One more thing: `attn = attn.masked_fill(mask == 0, float("-inf"))`. The mask is Boolean or float. In our kernel, we check `mask_ptr[j] == 0.0f`. This matches. But what if `mask_ptr` contains values that are not exactly 0 or 1, e.g., 0.5? In the reference, `msa_mask` is typically a binary mask, but it might be float. The reference does `== 0`, which checks exact zero. So checking `== 0.0f` is correct. Another issue: shared memory bank conflicts. We stored `x_shared` as `x_shared[k * S + s]`. That transposes input so that threads in a warp accessing the same `s` but different `k` get contiguous memory. However, in the projection step, each thread in a warp handles different `idx` which maps to different `h,s,dd`. For a given `idx`, when we loop over `k`, we access `x_shared[k * S + s]` with stride `S` between consecutive `k`. If threads in a warp have the same `s` but different `h` or `dd`, they access different `k * S + s` which differ by `S` in `k`. This might cause bank conflicts if `S` (8) is a multiple of bank width (4 bytes). Actually shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes. `x_shared[k*S+s]` with consecutive `k` differ by `S` floats = `S*4` bytes. If S=8, then `8*4=32` bytes = 8 banks apart. Threads in a warp accessing same `s` but different `k` will have addresses 8 banks apart, so no conflict? Let's see: thread i accesses bank `(addr / 4) % 32`. For addresses `k*S + s`, with S=8, the bank is `(k*8 + s) % 32`. Consecutive threads may have different `k`. If threads access `k=0..31` with same `s`, the banks are `s, s+8, s+16, s+24, s+0, s+8, ...` So each thread accesses one of 4 banks, which means 8 threads per bank -> bank conflicts (8-way). That's bad but maybe not catastrophic for this small kernel since the computations are memory bound. But we can optimize by using float4 loads or by changing the layout. However, for now, the main priority is correctness and compilation. Also, need to ensure we handle the `math.sqrt(d)` correctly: we used `sqrtf((float)d)`, which is fine. Now, the kernel may fail correctness test due to the differences. Let's try compiling first. Another issue I spotted: in the output projection, `out_shared` still stores data in `(h, S, d)` order. We read from `out_shared[(h * S + s) * d + dd]`. That is correct. Wait, is `out_shared` layout `(h, S, d)`? We wrote `out_shared[idx] = sum * g_shared[idx]` where `idx` ranges linearly over `H * S * d`. We didn't permute before writing to `out_shared`. However, the `out_shared` at that stage has shape `(H, S, d)` with each `idx = (h * S + s) * d + dd`. That's correct. So output projection accesses `out_shared[(h * S + s) * d + dd]`. Yes. Now, check `out_shared` storage: `g_shared + H * S * d` points to `attn_shared`. Then `out_shared` is `attn_shared + H * S * S`. So `out_shared` has length `H * S * d`. Good. Now, fix the compilation. Let's write a new kernel code with `cpp_sources` containing declaration. One more micro-optimization: we can reduce dynamic shared memory size. But it's fine. Let's try.

But wait, PyTorch linear weights are `(out_features, in_features)`. So `weight[k, d_in]` is actually `weight[out_idx, in_idx]`. When transposed, `weight_t[in_idx, out_idx]`. Row-major indexing of `weight_t` is `in_idx * out_features + out_idx`. So `k * D + (h*d+dd)` is correct. However, for `x_shared[k * S + s]`, we are accumulating over `k` (input dim). The thread `idx` iterates over output dims `(h, s, dd)`. So for a fixed `s`, threads with different `h, dd` all read `x_shared` with varying `k`. This means within a warp, threads diverge on `k` loop but that's okay because they all read the same `x_shared[k * S + s]` at each `k`. Actually, the `k` loop is serial, so all threads read `x_shared[k * S + s]` for the same `k` sequentially. This is coalesced? Wait, `x_shared` is in shared memory, so bank conflicts only matter. At step `k`, all threads read `x_shared[k * S + s]`. Different threads have different `s`. So they read different addresses. Shared memory banks on A100/H100 are typically 32 banks for 32-bit words, interleaved. Address `k*S + s` maps to bank `(k*S + s) % 32`. Since `S=8`, bank is `(k*8 + s) % 32`. For a warp of 32 threads, if the threads span 32 different `s`, only 8 unique `s` exist. So it's only 8-way conflict if they span different `s` values. But in the loop, `idx` advances by `num_threads`. If `num_threads=128`, then for a given `idx`, the warp threads have `s` values that might differ by 1 or more. Since `S=8`, for `d=16`, `S*d=128`. So `total_proj = 4*8*16 = 512`. `threads=128`. For `idx` from `tid` to `tid + 31` (a warp), `idx` increases by 1 across lanes. `h = idx / 128`, `rem = idx % 128`, `s = rem / 16`, `dd = rem % 16`. So within a warp of 32 threads: - `idx` spans 32 values. - `s` spans `32/16 = 2` values. - So 16 threads read `s`, 16 read `s+1`. At step `k`, they read `x_shared[k*S + s]` and `x_shared[k*S + s+1]`. These are two different banks. 16 threads hit one bank, 16 hit another. For shared memory, 16-way bank conflict. This is slow! A better layout for `x_shared` that avoids bank conflicts when multiple threads read different `s` but same `k` is to swap the dimensions: `x_shared[s * D + k]`. In that case, address is `s*D + k`. Bank is `(s*D + k) % 32`. If `D=64`, then `s=0,1` map to `(k)%32` and `(64+k)%32 = (k)%32` (since 64 mod 32 = 0). Both map to the SAME bank! Wait, `64 % 32 = 0`, so `s*D + k` for `s=0` and `s=1` gives same bank for same `k`. That would be a 16-way conflict between `s=0` and `s=1` threads. No, bank for `s=0, k` is `k%32`. Bank for `s=1, k` is `(64+k)%32 = k%32`. Yes, same bank! That's even worse. The transpose I originally chose: `x_shared[k * S + s]`. Address bank: `(k*S + s) % 32` = `(k*8 + s) % 32`. For `s=0..7`, `(k*8+s)%32` are all different IF `s` range fits in a stride of 1 and `8` is coprime to 32 (gcd(8,32)=8). So with stride 8, after 4 values of `s`, the bank repeats. `s=0`: bank `k*8 % 32` `s=1`: bank `(k*8+1)%32` ... `s=7`: bank `(k*8+7)%32` `s=8`: bank `(k*8+8)%32 = (k*8)%32 + 8`, but `s` only goes to 7. So for `s=0..7`, the banks are `base, base+1, ..., base+7` (mod 32). They are all distinct! Because `base = (k*8)%32`. Adding 1..7 to base doesn't wrap around mod 32 within 8 steps. So for a warp reading 32 consecutive `idx`, which span 2 `s` values (say `s` and `s+1`), 16 threads read bank `base+s` and 16 read `base+s+1`. These are distinct banks. So no bank conflict! Wait, but 16 threads reading the SAME bank is a 16-way bank conflict. Here, 16 threads read ONE bank, and 16 read ANOTHER bank. So two banks are accessed by 16 threads each. That is a 16-way conflict per bank. So there IS a conflict. How to fix? We can use `x_shared` with strided layout `x_shared[s + k * S]` is what I have. If we instead make `x_shared` storing `x_shared[D * s + k]`, bank = `(64*s + k)%32 = (k)%32`. So all threads reading different `s` but same `k` hit the SAME bank. Disaster. What if we use `x_shared[k + D * s]` and add padding? Like `x_shared[s * (D + 1) + k]`. Bank = `((D+1)*s + k) % 32 = (65*s + k)%32`. Since `65 % 32 = 1`, bank = `(s + k) % 32`. For a warp with 16 threads at `s` and 16 at `s+1`, banks are `(s+k)%32` and `(s+1+k)%32`. For the 16 threads at same `s`, they all have DIFFERENT `k`? No, within a warp of 32 threads spanning 2 s values, `k` is the same across threads because they are iterating over `k` serially in the outermost loop. In the projection loop: ```cpp for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xv = x_shared[...]; // all threads in warp read same k but different s ... } ``` So at step k, all threads in a warp read the same `k`. Thus if x_shared bank depends on `s`, we want `s` to map to different banks for the threads within the warp. My current layout: `x_shared[k * S + s]`, bank = `(k*8 + s) % 32`. For the 16 threads at `s`, bank = `(k*8 + s) % 32`. 16 threads hitting the SAME bank. That's a 16-way conflict. With padded layout `x_shared[s * (D+PAD) + k]`, bank = `(s*(D+PAD) + k) % 32`. If `D+PAD = 65`, bank = `(s + k) % 32`. For 16 threads at same `s`, still same bank! Because `s` is the same for those 16 threads. Wait, within the warp, there are 16 threads for `s` and 16 for `s+1`. The 16 threads with same `s` all have the same `s`, so they access the same bank regardless of padding! Oh, they have different `idx` but same `s` and same `k` (because `k` is the loop variable). So yes, 16 threads access the same address `x_shared[s][k]`! Actually, they access the same exact word. In shared memory, when all threads in a warp read the same word, it's a broadcast, NO bank conflict! Wait, is it a broadcast? If they read the same address, it's a broadcast, not a conflict. Let me re-evaluate. In the projection loop: - Threads within a warp have `idx` from `tid` to `tid+31`. - Since `total_proj` is iterated with `idx += num_threads`, but for a single iteration of the `for` loop over `idx`, threads `tid..tid+31` are active. - `h = idx / (S*d)`, `rem = idx % (S*d)`, `s = rem / d`, `dd = rem % d`. - `S*d = 128`. So for `idx` in `[0, 127]`, `h=0`. For `idx` in `[128, 255]`, `h=1`, etc. - Within a warp of 32 threads, `idx` spans 32 values. - If warp starts at `idx=0`, it covers `idx=0..31`. `s = idx / 16` (integer division), so `s=0` for `idx=0..15`, `s=1` for `idx=16..31`. `dd = idx % 16`, so for `s=0`, `dd=0..15`, for `s=1`, `dd=0..15`. - All threads in the warp have DIFFERENT `idx`, but at step `k`, they all read `x_shared[k * S + s]`. - For threads with `s=0`, they all read `x_shared[k * S + 0]`. - For threads with `s=1`, they all read `x_shared[k * S + 1]`. - So there are 2 distinct addresses being read by the warp. Each address is read by 16 threads. - In shared memory, when multiple threads read the SAME address, it's a broadcast (no conflict). When threads read different addresses in the same bank, that's a conflict. - Since `x_shared[k*S+0]` and `x_shared[k*S+1]` are in different banks (because stride is 1 byte offset? No, bank is determined by (address/4)%32. Address difference is 4 bytes, so banks differ by 1 mod 32), these are in different banks. So 16 threads access bank B, 16 access bank B+1. This is NOT a conflict. Bank conflicts only happen when multiple threads access DIFFERENT addresses in the SAME bank. Here, 16 threads access the SAME address in one bank (broadcast), and 16 access the SAME address in another bank (broadcast). So ZERO bank conflicts for `x_shared` reads! Great. Wait, I need to double-check. In shared memory, bank width is 4 bytes. Address `A` is in bank `(A/4) % 32`. Address of `x_shared[k*S + s]` is `base + (k*S + s)*4`. Bank = `((base/4) + k*S + s) % 32`. For `s=0`: bank = `C + k*S (mod 32)`. For `s=1`: bank = `C + k*S + 1 (mod 32)`. These are different. And all 16 threads hitting `s=0` are hitting the same address. That is a broadcast. So no bank conflict. Good. Now, what about `w_q` reads? `widx = k * D + h * d + dd`. At step `k`, all threads in warp read `w_q[k*D + offset]`. The `offset = h*d + dd` is different for each thread. So they read `w_q` at addresses spaced by `d=16` elements (since `h` changes every 128 threads, within a warp `h` is usually constant, as warp covers 32 idx, and `S*d=128`). Within a warp (32 threads), `h` is constant. `dd` ranges over 16 values (0..15), then `s` changes. Wait, `idx=0..31` for first warp: `dd = idx%16`, so `dd` goes 0..15 twice (for `s=0` and `s=1`). So addresses are `k*D + h*d + dd` where `dd` ranges 0..15. These are 16 consecutive floating-point values (since `dd` increments by 1). And 16 threads access the first set (`s=0`), and 16 access the second set (`s=1`) with same `dd`s. So 16 threads access address `A`, and 16 access address `A` again. So it's two broadcasts of the same address across half-warp? No, both halves access the exact same addresses for the same `dd`. So for `dd=0`, 2 threads (one in `s=0` half, one in `s=1` half) access `w_q[k*D + h*d + 0]`. Wait, `s` doesn't appear in the `w_q` index! So threads with same `dd` but different `s` access the SAME address in `w_q`. Let's map it: - `idx=0`: `dd=0` - `idx=1`: `dd=1` - ... - `idx=15`: `dd=15` - `idx=16`: `dd=0` - ... - `idx=31`: `dd=15` So for each `dd` in 0..15, exactly 2 threads (with `s=0` and `s=1`) access the same `w_q` address. This is a broadcast for each `dd` (2 threads same address). No bank conflict. This is all fine. What about writes to `q_shared`, etc.? `q_shared[idx]` where `idx` goes from `tid` to `tid+31` consecutively. These are consecutive addresses in shared memory. That would normally cause conflicts, but with 32 threads writing 32 consecutive floats, each float is in a different bank (assuming bank width 4 bytes). Address `base + idx*4` maps to bank `(base/4 + idx) % 32`. This is a perfect stride-1 access with NO bank conflicts! Great. So the shared memory layout is actually quite efficient. What about attention score computation? ```cpp for (int idx = tid; idx < total_attn; idx += num_threads) { int h = idx / (S * S); int rem = idx % (S * S); int i = rem / S; int j = rem % S; ... q_shared[(h * S + i) * d + dd] * k_shared[(h * S + j) * d + dd] } ``` Here, `idx` is over `H*S*S = 4*8*8 = 256`. `threads=128`. So each thread handles 2 elements. For `idx`, `h` and `i,j` vary. The inner loop over `dd=0..15` reads from `q_shared` and `k_shared`. In a warp, threads have different `idx`. So they have different `h, i, j`. At step `dd`, thread reads `q_shared[(h*S+i)*d + dd]`. Since `h*S+i` differs among threads, the addresses differ. `dd` is the same for all threads at this step. Address in `q_shared`: `base + ((h*S+i)*d + dd)*4`. Bank: `(base/4 + (h*S+i)*d + dd) % 32`. Since `d=16`, `(h*S+i)*d` is a multiple of 16. `S=8`, `h=0..3`, `i=0..7`. `(h*S+i)` ranges 0..31. Times 16 gives multiples of 16 from 0..496. Bank = `(base/4 + multiple_of_16 + dd) % 32`. For a warp, threads have different `(h*S+i)`. The multiples of 16 modulo 32 are either 0 or 16. If `(h*S+i)` is even, multiple is `even*16`. 16*even is multiple of 32 -> bank 0 + dd. If `(h*S+i)` is odd, 16*odd = 16 (mod 32) -> bank 16 + dd. So threads are either in bank `dd` or `16+dd`. If `dd=0..15`, `dd` and `16+dd` are distinct. But multiple threads could map to the same bank. Specifically, for a fixed `dd`, threads with even `(h*S+i)` all map to bank `dd`. Threads with odd `(h*S+i)` map to bank `16+dd`. How many threads in a warp have even `(h*S+i)`? It depends on warp composition. But this is a potential bank conflict in `q_shared` and `k_shared` reads. This could be optimized by transposing `q_shared` so that `dd` is the fast dimension and the index is `dd * (H*S) + (h*S+i)`. Address: `base + (dd * (H*S) + (h*S+i)) * 4`. Bank: `(base/4 + dd*32 + (h*S+i)) % 32 = (base/4 + (h*S+i)) % 32`. Because `H*S = 32`. So `dd*32` is a multiple of 32. Then bank depends only on `(h*S+i)`. For a warp, `(h*S+i)` could be same for multiple threads -> conflict. Actually, in this version, threads with the same `(h*S+i)` have the same bank, and there are `S` threads with the same `h,i` but different `j` (since `idx` spans `j`). In a warp of 32 threads, we might have 32 different `(h*S+i,j)` pairs. But for a fixed `dd` step, if two threads have the same `(h*S+i)`, they hit the same bank. The number of such collisions depends on the warp. Actually, maybe this is not a huge bottleneck yet because the problem size is very small. The total shared memory and computation is tiny. reasoning
The profiling shows two main issues: 1. There's a lot of overhead from `.contiguous()`, `.clone()`, `.copy_` operations. I should avoid unnecessary transposes/contiguous calls. 2. The kernel itself is latency-bound with lots of uncoalesced loads. The global load coalescing is very poor (182.4x wasted). The main problem in my kernel is that weights are accessed in a strided manner. When I compute Q, K, V, G: ``` int widx = k * D + h * d + dd; ``` This means each adjacent thread with different `s` is accessing the same weight memory, but adjacent load instructions from the same thread are strided by `D`. Since `D = 64`, this is not terrible (16 4-byte elements = 64 bytes), but still not great. The bigger problem is that `w_q[widx]` isn't contiguous per thread - each thread loads `w_q[0], w_q[64], w_q[128]...` which means the loads from a single thread are strided by 64 floats (256 bytes), causing many cache lines to be fetched but only one float used per line. Wait, actually let me reconsider. Warp load instructions are issued per-warp. If all threads in a warp access the same address, that's a broadcast and fine. But if they access different addresses, they need to be contiguous for coalescing. In my loop over `k`: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { float xv = x_shared[k * S + s]; int widx = k * D + h * d + dd; sum_q += xv * w_q[widx]; } ``` For a fixed `k`, threads with same `s` but different `h,d` access different `widx`. The threads in a warp likely have different `h` and `dd`. So they access different weight addresses strided by `d` or `d*S`. This is not coalesced. Actually, the occupancy and parallelism issues are also severe: 70.7% occupancy with 1152 threads per block (block=1152) and 211 regs/thread. That's 3 blocks per SM at most (128KB registers / 211*1152 ≈ 0.5 blocks, so limited by registers to 1 block). On H100 with 64 warps per SM and 1152 threads = 36 warps, that gives 70% occupancy. Wait - the profile says "block=1152" but I set `threads=128`. Let me re-read... The profile says block=1152. That doesn't match my kernel. Oh, wait - those might be from the `aten` operations (the contiguous copies, layer_norm, etc.). My kernel might not even be registered as a separate kernel in the profile because it's a custom inline-loaded extension. Profile tools might have trouble identifying it. Hmm, this is concerning. Let me check the actual timing. "GPU time: 230.59 μs" is the total GPU time. Let me see if the reference is faster or slower. Since I don't have the reference time from this tool, I can't directly compare. But if the kernel is correct and the overhead of copies dominates, I need to optimize the data layout. Actually, wait - the fact that `contiguous`, `clone`, `copy_` are the top 3 taking ~25% each suggests my Python-side `.t().contiguous()` and `.float()` are expensive. Key optimizations to try: 1. **Avoid `.t().contiguous()`**: If I prepare the weights in the right layout ahead of time, or access them transposed in the kernel. 2. **Avoid `.float()`**: If weights are already float (which they are for fp32), this is unnecessary. 3. **Weight layout for coalescing**: Store weights so that for a given output dimension and head, the input dimension varies fastest (which it already does, since nn.Linear stores as (out, in)). But I need to think about what makes coalesced loads. Actually, looking at PyTorch `nn.Linear` weight shape: `(out_features, in_features)`. For `query.weight` shape is `(D, D)`. When I do `.t()`, it becomes `(D, D)` with layout transposed logically but contiguous() makes it actually transposed in memory. Wait, the default PyTorch linear weight is already `(D_out, D_in)`. So `query.weight` is `(D, D)` with rows as output features. In the kernel, for each input feature `k`, I want all output features. If I keep the weight as-is (shape D, D) and read `w_q[h*d + dd, k]`, then for a fixed `k`, adjacent threads with different `h` or `dd` access adjacent memory! That would be coalesced. So instead of transposing the weights in Python, I should pass them as-is and adjust indexing in the kernel: ```cpp int widx = (h * d + dd) * D + k; // row-major: (out_idx, in_idx) ``` This is much better for coalescing! Let's fix this. Also, I should remove all the `.t().contiguous()` and `.float()` calls since they create unnecessary copies. Let me rewrite the kernel: 1. Pass weights in original layout 2. Fix indexing for coalesced loads 3. Remove unnecessary copies/transposes Also, for the output projection: `out_proj.weight` is `(D, D)`. In the kernel I have: ```cpp sum += val * w_out[d_in * D + d_out]; ``` This is currently row-major where `d_in` is the row. For fixed `d_out`, threads with different `d_in` access strided memory. If I change to `w_out[d_out * D + d_in]`, that's column-major for `d_in`. Wait no, I want for a fixed `d_out`, consecutive `d_in` accesses. But `d_in` is in the loop, each thread iterates `d_in`. The access pattern across threads within a warp matters. If all threads have the same `d_out` but different `s`, then `w_out[d_in * D + d_out]` - for a fixed `d_in` and `d_out`, all threads access the same address. That's fine (broadcast). The issue is just that `d_in` is iterated in a loop. But since all threads access the same address for a given `d_in`, there's no coalescing issue here - it's actually broadcast, which is efficient. Wait, but in the output projection: ```cpp for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { int h = d_in / d; int dd = d_in % d; float val = out_shared[(h * S + s) * d + dd]; sum += val * w_out[d_in * D + d_out]; } ``` For a fixed `d_in`, all threads with different `s` and same `d_out` access the same `w_out[d_in * D + d_out]`. This is broadcast, which is coalesced. And `out_shared[(h * S + s) * d + dd]` has adjacent `s` -> adjacent memory. So this is actually well-behaved! The main issue was in Q/K/V/G projection weights. Let me also think about the thread mapping to get better parallelism. Right now I use 1 block per `(b, n)` pair with 128 threads. The problem size is small: B=2, N=32, so 64 blocks total. Thread 0 loads x[0], x[1], x[2], x[3], etc. This is coalesced in the original (B, N, S, D) memory layout: x_ptr[s * D + k] where k is contiguous. So the original x loading was actually: ``` for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += num_threads) { int s = idx / D; int k = idx % D; x_shared[k * S + s] = x_ptr[s * D + k]; } ``` Here, x_ptr[s * D + k] with k being idx % D means consecutive threads access consecutive k values, which is contiguous in memory. So this IS coalesced. The weights access pattern was: ``` for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { int widx = k * D + h * d + dd; sum_q += xv * w_q[widx]; } ``` Here, for a fixed h and dd, consecutive k values access w_q at widx = k * D + constant, which has stride D=64. That's not contiguous across threads running different k in the same loop iteration. But since all threads in a warp will have the same h and dd but potentially different s, they're all reading different w_q addresses but same k in lockstep. Actually within a warp, if threads have different s, they'll all access w_q with the same widx within one iteration of k! Because widx doesn't depend on s. So all threads in a warp read the SAME address for w_q[k * D + h * d + dd]. That's a broadcast, which is actually efficient (it gets cached, or it uses uniform memory access). This is good for both x and W access if x is stored as x[s, in] and W is stored as W[out, in]. For multiple threads in a warp computing the same s but different out, they all read the same x[s][in] sequence but different W[out][in] sequences. The W reads will be strided by D=64 between consecutive out values. If 32 threads in a warp compute out=0,1,2,...,31, then at step in, they read W_q[0*D+in], W_q[1*D+in], ..., W_q[31*D+in]. These have a stride of D floats = 256 bytes, which means each warp reads 32 values with 256-byte stride. On modern GPUs with L1 cache, this might still be okay. Actually, 256 bytes is exactly 4 cache lines of 64 bytes each, so 4 transactions per warp per read. That's not great but not terrible. If threads compute consecutive out values, the memory access for W is strided by D. A better approach: have threads compute the same out but different s values? But then x access is strided. Consecutive out with fixed s: W is strided by D. Consecutive s with fixed out: x is strided by D. Both have the same striding issue. But we can tile it differently: have a warp compute a tile of (s_block, out_block), and load tiles into shared memory, then do the matmul in shared memory. For D=64 and S=8, the simplest is to have each thread compute one element, and rely on the L1 cache. Since each (s, out) element requires reading 64 floats from x and 64 from W, the total per element is 512 bytes. Even with cache misses, it should be fine for this small problem. Let me write the complete kernel now. Actually, I realize my previous kernel had a bug I didn't notice. In the reference: ```python q = self.query(x).view(B, N, S, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4) ``` This gives q shape (B, N, H, S, d). The attention computes over the S dimension: ```python attn = torch.einsum("bnhid,bnhjd->bnhij", q, k) / math.sqrt(d) ``` So in my kernel, the Q buffer should be Q[h, i, dd] = sum_k x[k] * W_q[h*d+dd, k]. The attention is then Q[h, i, :] @ K[h, j, :]^T. This is what I implemented before. Let me keep this structure but make it cleaner. For the updated kernel with fused ln: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float warpReduceSum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); return val; } __device__ __forceinline__ float blockReduceSum(float val) { __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warpReduceSum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); return val; } __global__ void fused_msa_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ msa_repr, const float* __restrict__ msa_mask, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta_ln, const float* __restrict__ w_q, const float* __restrict__ w_k, const float* __restrict__ w_v, const float* __restrict__ w_g, const float* __restrict__ b_g, const float* __restrict__ w_out, const float* __restrict__ b_out, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int D, int H, float eps) { int d_head = D / H; int bn = blockIdx.x; int b = bn / N; int n = bn % N; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; const float NEG_INF = __int_as_float(0xFF800000); extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; // S * D float* q_smem = x_smem + S * D; // H * S * d_head float* k_smem = q_smem + H * S * d_head; float* v_smem = k_smem + H * S * d_head; float* g_smem = v_smem + H * S * d_head; float* a_smem = g_smem + H * S * d_head; // H * S * S float* o_smem = a_smem + H * S * S; // H * S * d_head // 1. Load x from global (B, S, N, D) -> x_smem layout: row-major [s][d] for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += nthreads) { int s = idx / D; int d = idx % D; x_smem[idx] = msa_repr[((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + d]; } __syncthreads(); // 2. LayerNorm per row s // Compute mean for each s using parallel reduction for (int s = 0; s < S; ++s) { float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += nthreads) { local_sum += x_smem[s * D + d]; } float total_sum = blockReduceSum(local_sum); __shared__ float mean_val; if (tid == 0) mean_val = total_sum * (1.0f / D); __syncthreads(); float local_sq = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += nthreads) { float diff = x_smem[s * D + d] - mean_val; local_sq += diff * diff; } float total_sq = blockReduceSum(local_sq); __shared__ float inv_std; if (tid == 0) inv_std = rsqrtf(total_sq * (1.0f / D) + eps); __syncthreads(); for (int d = tid; d < D; d += nthreads) { float val = (x_smem[s * D + d] - mean_val) * inv_std; val = val * gamma[d] + beta_ln[d]; x_smem[s * D + d] = val; } __syncthreads(); } // 3. Q, K, V, G projections // Each output element is (h, s, dd) int total_qkvg = H * S * d_head; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_qkvg; idx += nthreads) { int h = idx / (S * d_head); int rem = idx % (S * d_head); int s = rem / d_head; int dd = rem % d_head; int out_dim = h * d_head + dd; float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f, sum_g = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float xv = x_smem[s * D + d]; int widx = out_dim * D + d; sum_q += xv * w_q[widx]; sum_k += xv * w_k[widx]; sum_v += xv * w_v[widx]; sum_g += xv * w_g[widx]; } q_smem[idx] = sum_q; k_smem[idx] = sum_k; v_smem[idx] = sum_v; float gv = sum_g + b_g[out_dim]; g_smem[idx] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gv)); } __syncthreads(); // 4. Attention scores: for each h, i, j int total_attn = H * S * S; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_attn; idx += nthreads) { int h = idx / (S * S); int rem = idx % (S * S); int i = rem / S; int j = rem % S; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d_head; ++dd) { sum += q_smem[(h * S + i) * d_head + dd] * k_smem[(h * S + j) * d_head + dd]; } a_smem[idx] = sum * rsqrtf((float)d_head); } __syncthreads(); // 5. Mask and softmax for (int idx = tid; idx < H * S; idx += nthreads) { int h = idx / S; int i = idx % S; // Find max float max_val = NEG_INF; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (msa_mask[((b * S + j) * N + n)] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } // Sum exp float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (msa_mask[((b * S + j) * N + n)] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; sum_exp += expf(val - max_val); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Normalize #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j]; if (msa_mask[((b * S + j) * N + n)] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF; a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j] = expf(val - max_val) * inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // 6. Weighted sum with V, then gate multiply for (int idx = tid; idx < total_qkvg; idx += nthreads) { int h = idx / (S * d_head); int rem = idx % (S * d_head); int i = rem / d_head; int dd = rem % d_head; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { sum += a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j] * v_smem[(h * S + j) * d_head + dd]; } o_smem[idx] = sum * g_smem[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // 7. Output projection: (H, S, d_head) -> (S, D) for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D; idx += nthreads) { int s = idx / D; int dd_out = idx % D; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { int h = d_in / d_head; int dd_in = d_in % d_head; sum += o_smem[(h * S + s) * d_head + dd_in] * w_out[d_in * D + dd_out]; } sum += b_out[dd_out]; out[((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + dd_out] = sum; } } ``` One issue: `blockReduceSum` uses `shared[32]`, but when blockDim.x < 32, wid is always 0 and lane is threadIdx.x. Instead, use a shared memory array and a single tree reduction: ``` __shared__ float smem_reduce[256]; // or blockDim.x // Each thread computes sum for all s float sum_all_s[8] = {0}; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += nthreads) { for (int s = 0; s < S; s++) { sum_all_s[s] += x_smem[s * D + d]; } } for (int s = 0; s < S; s++) { smem_reduce[tid] = sum_all_s[s]; __syncthreads(); // tree reduce for (int stride = nthreads/2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) smem_reduce[tid] += smem_reduce[tid + stride]; __syncthreads(); } float mean = smem_reduce[0] * (1.0f / D); // use mean... } ``` This uses fewer instructions and avoids the warp shuffle. But with nthreads=256, the tree reduction has log2(256)=8 steps with 8 syncs. And we do it S=8 times = 64 syncs. That's more syncs than before. Hmm. Maybe the warp shuffle approach is better. Actually, let me use a different `blockReduceSum` that doesn't use `__syncthreads()`: ``` __device__ __forceinline__ float blockReduceSumNoSync(float val, float* shared) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warpReduceSum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); if (wid == 0) { val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; val = warpReduceSum(val); } __syncthreads(); return val; } ``` This includes an extra __syncthreads() at the end. But if I call it in a loop, I need __syncthreads() after anyway, so I can use this version. But wait, if only `wid == 0` computes the final value, then only threads in warp 0 have the correct return value. I need to broadcast. Or simpler: have all threads participate in loading the result: ``` __device__ __forceinline__ float blockReduceSumBroadcast(float val, float* shared) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warpReduceSum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); // broadcast to all if (threadIdx.x == 0) shared[0] = val; __syncthreads(); return shared[0]; } ``` This returns the same value for all threads. But it adds 2 more __syncthreads(). For our small problem, this is fine. Actually, looking at CUDA examples, a simpler block reduce: ``` __device__ __forceinline__ float blockReduceSum(float val) { static __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warpReduceSum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); return val; } ``` And then in the caller: ``` float sum = blockReduceSum(local_sum); if (threadIdx.x == 0) mean_val = sum * (1.0f / D); __syncthreads(); ``` Since only thread 0 writes mean_val, and all threads read it after __syncthreads(), we're good. Other threads have garbage in `sum`, but they don't use it. This is what I had originally. Let me keep it. OK I'll write the final code now. Let me also handle the mask type properly - I'll pass it as float. For the main kernel, I'll use `nthreads = 256` and compute shared memory needed. SMEM = S * D (x) + 4 * H * S * d_head (q, k, v, g) + H * S * S (attn) + H * S * d_head (output after gate) + 32 (block reduce scratch) = 512 + 2048 + 256 + 512 + 32 = 3360 floats = 13440 bytes Plus some extra for mean/var: + 2 * S = 16 floats = 64 bytes Total ~ 13504 bytes, which is < 48KB limit. Actually wait, I need mean_shared and var_shared or inv_std. Let me allocate: ``` __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; // S * D float* q_smem = x_smem + S * D; // H * S * d_head float* k_smem = q_smem + H * S * d_head; // H * S * d_head float* v_smem = k_smem + H * S * d_head; // H * S * d_head float* g_smem = v_smem + H * S * d_head; // H * S * d_head float* a_smem = g_smem + H * S * d_head; // H * S * S float* o_smem = a_smem + H * S * S; // H * S * d_head float* reduce_smem = o_smem + H * S * d_head; // 32 float* mean_smem = reduce_smem + 32; // S float* var_smem = mean_smem + S; // S ``` Total: 3328 + 32 + 8 + 8 = 3376 floats = 13504 bytes. Very safe. For the layer norm, I need a temporary place to store mean and inv_std. Since only thread 0 writes them, I can just use `__shared__ float mean_val; __shared__ float inv_std;` inside the kernel as scalars. That's simpler. Actually, `__shared__ float mean_val;` declared inside the kernel is fine in CUDA. All threads see the same shared variable. Let me now write the complete code and compile.

It performs LN per (s,n) across D, then Q,K,V,G, attn, softmax, V-weighted+gate, out_proj, and writes back directly. LayerNorm per row s\n for (int s = 0; s < S; ++s) {\n float local_sum = 0.0f;\n for (int d = tid; d < D; d += nthreads) {\n local_sum += x_smem[s * D + d];\n }\n float total_sum = blockReduceSum(local_sum);\n __shared__ float mean_val;\n if (tid == 0) mean_val = total_sum * (1.0f / D);\n __syncthreads();\n\n float local_sq = 0.0f;\n for (int d = tid; d < D; d += nthreads) {\n float diff = x_smem[s * D + d] - mean_val;\n local_sq += diff * diff;\n }\n float total_sq = blockReduceSum(local_sq);\n __shared__ float inv_std;\n if (tid == 0) inv_std = rsqrtf(total_sq * (1.0f / D) + eps);\n __syncthreads();\n\n for (int d = tid; d < D; d += nthreads) {\n float val = (x_smem[s * D + d] - mean_val) * inv_std;\n val = val * gamma[d] + beta_ln[d];\n x_smem[s * D + d] = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n // 3. turn 9llm latency: 43.5s
reasoning
The kernel performance improved from 230.59μs to 75.62μs. However, there are several issues: 1. Most of the time is still spent in PyTorch's contiguous operations (32.6ms on average). The actual CUDA kernel is only 75.62μs. 2. Coalescing is terrible: 162x wasted BW. The issue is that we're loading weights in a very non-coalesced way. Every thread loads a different column of the weights. The main issue is the weight loading pattern. We're using block-level parallelization where all threads in a block load a specific output dimension. This means threads access memory with stride D (64), which is highly uncoalesced. To fix this, I need to restructure the weight access pattern or use per-warp computations where within a warp, threads access contiguous memory. For a small D=64, S=32, H=4, the best approach is to leverage cuBLAS for the matrix multiplications and just fuse the softmax/mask/gate operations. But the overhead of cuBLAS might be significant for such small matrices. Let me think about another approach. We can use Tensor Cores with TF32 matmul! The dimensions are D=64, S=32, which are small but might still be faster on H100. Actually, the easiest and most maintainable approach would be to let PyTorch handle the linear projections (which it already does well), and only fuse the attention scoring and softmax parts into a custom kernel. This way, we avoid the uncoalesced weight loading issues entirely. Let me design a hybrid approach: 1. Use PyTorch native for Q, K, V, G linear projections and layer norm. 2. Fused kernel just for: attention scores → mask → softmax → weighted sum → gate multiply. 3. Use PyTorch native for output projection. This avoids all weight loading issues and just processes the Q, K, V, G, and mask in a fused kernel. Wait, let me check the reference timing to see where it spends time. Prof showed GPU time, but that's for our kernel. Let me understand: the reference likely takes ~100-200μs. With our 75μs kernel plus the contiguous/clones overhead (~11ms each), we're actually still SLOWER than the reference because of the overhead. Actually wait - looking at the profile more carefully: `aten::contiguous` takes ~10.8ms, but those are single calls. That seems like it might be CPU time, not GPU time? Or perhaps it's cudaMemcpy time for converting transposed to contiguous. Let me check if these are synchronous or async. Actually, those times (10.8ms) are way too large for a single copy of 64*32*32*4=262KB. Something might be misleading in the profiler output. Regardless, we need to reduce memory copies. The main problem is the `.contiguous()` and `.t()` calls. These create new tensors and copy data around. For 64x64 matrices, the transpose copy is very fast but cuBLAS might incur synchronization. Let me measure the reference timing first. Actually, I don't have a direct tool for that, but I can assume the reference is around 100-200μs based on typical PyTorch performance for these small dimensions. I need to minimize host-side overhead. The best way is to avoid any `.contiguous()` and `.t()` calls in the forward pass. With native PyTorch, the weights are stored as (out_features, in_features), so for matmul we need (in_features, out_features). We can either: 1. Store weights pre-transposed in the model (but this changes the model state structure) 2. Use PyTorch matmul which handles the transposition automatically Actually, the simplest fix is to NOT fuse the linear projections into CUDA, but instead use PyTorch's built-in linear ops (which handle transposition efficiently via cuBLAS) and only fuse the attention computation. Let me write a hybrid kernel: 1. LayerNorm + Q/K/V/G projections via PyTorch (already done efficiently) 2. Fused kernel: takes Q, K, V, G, mask → returns gated attention output 3. Output projection via PyTorch linear This eliminates the need for transposing and copying weight tensors. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def forward(self, msa_repr, msa_mask): B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape x = msa_repr.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() # (B, N, S, D) x = self.layer_norm(x) # (B, N, S, D) q = self.query(x) # (B, N, S, D) k = self.key(x) v = self.value(x) g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)) # Fused attention kernel takes Q,K,V,G,mask # and returns (g * softmax(QK^T/sqrt(d)) V) in (B, N, S, D) out = fused_attention_kernel(q, k, v, g, msa_mask.permute(0, 2, 1)) out = self.out_proj(out) return out.permute(0, 2, 1, 3) ``` The main advantage is: - PyTorch handles the linear algebra efficiently with cuBLAS - The attention kernel is small and doesn't need weight data - No weight transpose overhead For the attention kernel, Q/K/V/G are (B, N, S, D) in global memory. We can process one (B, N) element per block. Within a block, each thread handles one (S, d_head) element. But actually, cuBLAS with batched matrix multiply is already very fast for Q @ K^T of shape (B*N*H, S, d) @ (B*N*H, S, d)^T. For small sizes like S=32, d=16, it might still be fast. And then softmax is a separate kernel. The performance win might come from simply removing overheads, not from fusing everything. However, for S=32, the attention computation is trivial. A custom kernel that loads Q,K,V into shared memory and does everything in one go is probably faster than multiple cuBLAS calls. Let me write a simpler attention-only fused kernel that just does: 1. Load Q, K, V, G for one (b, n) into shared memory 2. Compute attention scores 3. Mask + softmax 4. Weighted sum + gate 5. Write output directly This avoids all weight loading issues and the kernel is very simple. For even more performance, I could use CUTLASS or WMMA for the small attention matmul. But let me first do the simple version. Actually, let me try the approach where I use PyTorch for everything except I fuse the attention score → softmax → multiply in one kernel. Even better: use `torch.matmul` for Q@K^T and P@V, and fuse softmax + gating. Let me try the pure PyTorch approach first to see how fast it is. I can't benchmark it directly, but I can write a simpler fused kernel and see. For the attention-only kernel, I need: - Inputs: q, k, v, g all (B, N, S, D), mask (B, N, S) - Output: (B, N, S, D) The kernel per block: 1. Write out With D=64, S=32, total shared memory = ~24KB, well within limits. Since we don't need to load weights, the loads are perfectly coalesced! const int n = bn % N;\n const int tid = threadIdx.x;\n const int nthreads = blockDim.x;\n const float NEG_INF = __int_as_float(0xFF800000);\n const float scale = rsqrtf((float)d_head);\n\n extern rem = idx % (S * S);\n int i = rem / S;\n int j = rem % S;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < d_head; ++d) {\n sum += q_smem[(h * S + i) * d_head + d] * k_smem[(h * S + j) * d_head + d];\n }\n a_smem[idx] = sum * scale;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Mask + softmax per (h, i) over j\n for (int idx = tid; idx < H * S; idx += nthreads) {\n int h = idx / S;\n int i = idx % max_val);\n }\n float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) {\n float val = a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j];\n if (mask[bn * S + j] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF;\n a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j] = expf(val - max_val) * inv_sum;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Weighted sum: each thread computes one (h, i, d)\n int total_out = H * S * d_head;\n for (int idx = tid; idx < total_out; idx += nthreads) {\n int h = idx / (S * d_head);\n int rem = idx % (S * d_head);\n int i = rem / d_head;\n int d = rem % d_head;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < g_smem[out_idx];\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fused_attention_forward(\n torch::Tensor q,\n torch::Tensor k,\n torch::Tensor v,\n torch::Tensor g,\n torch::Tensor mask,\n int num_heads\n) {\n int B = q.size(0);\n int N = q.size(1);\n int S = q.size(2);\n int D = q.size(3);\n int H = num_heads;\n auto out = torch::empty_like(q);\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = B * N;\n size_t smem_size = (4 * S mask.data_ptr<float>(),\n out.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, N, S, D, H\n );\n return out;\n}\n'''\n\n_fused_cuda = load_inline(\n name='fused_attention_only',\n cpp_sources=cpp_src,\n cuda_sources=cuda_src,\n functions=['fused_attention_forward'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, msa_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0):\n super().__init__()\n self.num_heads = num_heads\n self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads\n assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0\n\n self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)\n So for d_in=0, thread0 reads w_q[0*D+0], thread1 reads w_q[1*D+0]. These are strided by D, which is 64 floats = 256 bytes. A cache line is 128 bytes, so threads in a warp access every other cache line. This is NOT fully coalesced! With 32 threads, they'd access 32 different cache lines, causing 32 sectors. That's bad! Oh! This is the source of the high sectors/req! In the projection loop, within a warp, consecutive threads have consecutive `out_dim`. They access `w_q[out_dim * D + d_in]` for the same `d_in`. So the addresses are spaced by D floats = 256 bytes. With 32 threads, that's 32 sectors per request. That's terrible coalescing! To fix this, we should transpose the weights (or equivalently, read them as `w_q[d_in * D + out_dim]`). This way, for a fixed `d_in`, consecutive threads access consecutive memory: `w_q[d_in*D + 0]`, `w_q[d_in*D + 1]`, etc. That IS coalesced! So we DO need the transposed weight layout for coalesced access! But in Python, we can pre-store transposed weights as persistent buffers in `__init__`, rather than calling `.t().contiguous()` on every forward. And we should do the same for all weight matrices. This way, CUDA gets coalesced loads and Python has zero overhead per forward. Similarly for the output projection. reasoning
The issue is that I used `self.register_buffer` with `detach()` in `__init__` but those won't get updated when the model parameters update during training. I need to pass the actual weights from the forward function instead, and I need to transpose them on the fly or pre-transpose them as buffers that get updated, but `register_buffer` with `.weight.detach()` won't track grad properly for `Linear.weight`. Actually, the weights are parameters of the Linear layers, so if I use `register_buffer` with a detached copy, it won't update with training. I should either: 1. Pass the weights directly from `forward()` and transpose them there (which still needs `.contiguous()`) 2. Use parameter hooks or update the buffers in forward(). Let me just transpose and contiguous-ify inside forward() to be safe, while avoiding the excessive contiguous/copy overhead. I can check if the weights are already Fortran-contiguous in one dimension. Actually for (D, D) matrix, `.t().contiguous()` creates a copy - but the weights are small (64x64). Also, I realized I need to verify correctness. But there's another issue: when I use `self.query.weight`, it is a parameter. I can create buffers but need to make sure they stay in sync. A simpler approach: just use the weights directly in forward() and call `.t().contiguous()`. The overhead is tiny for 64x64 matrices. But the profiling showed huge `aten::clone`/`aten::copy_` times — that was from the previous version where I did many contiguous() calls on the fly. Let me fix the forward() to not do unnecessary copies, and pass the raw weights. Actually, looking at the previous compile kernel that passed, the huge contiguous/clone/copy times are from the previous version where I called `.contiguous()` on everything in `forward()`. To avoid that, I should just pass the weights directly without contiguous(). The CUDA kernel reads them in a strided way anyway, so as long as the access pattern isn't terrible, it might be fine. But weight matrix access is `d_in * D + out_dim` which strides by D. If the weight is transposed but not contiguous, then `w_t[d_in * D + out_dim]` might have bad stride. So I should pass the original weights (not transposed) and adjust the indexing in the kernel to match the standard layout (out, in). Let me rewrite the kernel to accept weights in standard Linear format: `w[out, in]` of shape (D, D). In that case, access is `w[out_dim * D + d_in]`, which is fully contiguous across `d_in` if `d_in` is the inner loop. But in our kernel, `d_in` is the inner loop, so `widx = out_dim * D + d_in` is contiguous! So I should pass the weights untransposed and use `w[out_dim * D + d_in]`. This means no transpose needed at all in Python. The kernel will read weights coalesced if multiple threads access different `out_dim` but same `d_in` — wait, actually in our projection loop, threads process different `idx` (different `h, s, dd`), so `out_dim = h * d_head + dd` differs across threads. But `d_in` is the inner loop, so consecutive `d_in` values are accessed sequentially by each thread. Since `widx = out_dim * D + d_in`, consecutive `d_in` values are consecutive memory locations for each thread. This is very cache-friendly but not necessarily coalesced across threads. However, for small D=64, weights fit in L1/L2 cache, so this should be fine. For loading x: `x[s][d_in]` — threads with same `s` but different `d_in` access consecutive memory. If `tid` increments by 1 and `nthreads=128`, thread 0 loads x[s][0], thread 1 loads x[s][1], etc., which is coalesced. Good. For mask: `msa_mask[((b * S + j) * N + n)]` — all threads in a block access same mask element for same j, which is broadcast. Fine. For output: `out[((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + dd_out]` — threads writing different `dd_out` for same `s` are coalesced. Good. So the main issue with the previous version was unnecessary `.t().contiguous()` calls. Let me pass weights as-is and accept them in the kernel as `w` of shape (D, D) in row-major (out, in) layout. Wait, the kernel signature currently has `const float* __restrict__ w_q_t` etc. I need to change the indexing to `out_dim * D + d_in`. Let me create a fixed version.

1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gv));\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Attention scores Q @ K^T / sqrt(d)\n const int total_attn = H * S * S;\n for (int idx = tid; idx < total_attn; idx += nthreads) {\n int h = idx if (val > max_val) max_val = val;\n }\n float sum_exp = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) {\n float val = a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j];\n if (msa_mask[((b * S + j) * N + n)] == 0.0f) S + j) * N + n)] == 0.0f) val = NEG_INF;\n a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j] = expf(val - max_val) * inv_sum;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Weighted sum with V, then gate -> reuse q_smem for output\n for (int idx = tid; idx < total_qkvg; idx += nthreads) {\n int h = idx / (S * d_head);\n int rem = idx % (S * d_head);\n int i = rem / d_head;\n int dd = rem % d_head;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) {\n sum += a_smem[(h * S + i) * S + j] * v_smem[(h * S + j) * d_head + dd];\n }\n q_smem[idx] = sum * g_smem[idx];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Output projection (weights in (out, }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fused_msa_forward(\n torch::Tensor msa_repr,\n torch::Tensor msa_mask,\n torch::Tensor gamma,\n torch::Tensor beta_ln,\n torch::Tensor w_q,\n torch::Tensor w_k,\n torch::Tensor
